<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1578728782764242659">The basic strategy we use for raising children, teaching students, and managing workers can be summarized in six words: Do this and you'll get that. We dangle goodies (from candy bars to sales commissions) in front of people in much the same way we train the family pet. Drawing on a wealth of psychological research, Alfie Kohn points the way to a more successful strategy b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1578728782764242659">The basic strategy we use for raising children, teaching students, and managing workers can be summarized in six words: Do this and you'll get that. We dangle goodies (from candy bars to sales commissions) in front of people in much the same way we train the family pet. Drawing on a wealth of psychological research, Alfie Kohn points the way to a more successful strategy based on working with people instead of doing things to them. "Do rewards motivate people?" asks Kohn. "Yes. They motivate people to get rewards." Seasoned with humor and familiar examples, <em>Punished By Rewards</em> presents an argument unsettling to hear but impossible to dismiss.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1578728782764242659">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_509168246">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/509168246" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2450888-karin" class="left imgcol" title="Karin"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1345522311p2/2450888.jpg" alt="Karin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/509168246?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 19, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2450888-karin" class="user" title="Karin">Karin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2450888-karin?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2450888-karin?shelf=teaching-parenting" class="actionLinkLite">teaching-parenting</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2450888-karin?shelf=university-library" class="actionLinkLite">university-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer509168246">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12137798773407616353">My daughter's gr. 1 teacher just announced that if the kids read 100 books, they'd get a reward. Instead of being enthusiastic, or eager to read, DD just got upset and worried that now she can't get the reward because 100 seemed way too many. How different is 100 from a bazillion to a gr. 1 kid who barely knows how to count that high? <br /><br /> Reading this book helped me to understand that frustration, and non-interest is a normal reaction to bribery. Kohn states that kids and adults alike see both rewa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12137798773407616353">My daughter's gr. 1 teacher just announced that if the kids read 100 books, they'd get a reward. Instead of being enthusiastic, or eager to read, DD just got upset and worried that now she can't get the reward because 100 seemed way too many. How different is 100 from a bazillion to a gr. 1 kid who barely knows how to count that high? <br /><br /> Reading this book helped me to understand that frustration, and non-interest is a normal reaction to bribery. Kohn states that kids and adults alike see both rewards (incentive programs) and punishments as methods of controlling them. They tend to like the task less and work less hard to accomplish the project. This goes against society's ingrained belief that rewarding someone -whether by tangible items or praise motivates them to do better. He believes we should discuss things with kids, employees etc.&amp; work together to empower people no matter what age so they feel interested in the work they are doing. Kids can only learn to develop values and make decisions by being given the opportunities to practice and see the natural concequences of their own choices (age appropriately, of course.)<br /><br /><br /> My daughter does like to get a reward when she accomplishes something that's difficult for her! i guess i'm kind of working on the less rewards/ punishments and more explaining stuff to her. OTOH, how do you explain to a 6 yr old she can't stand outside the grocery store because in our society she could get picked up by an evil person. I don't want to scare her. I also didn't want her to find out the hard way that i was right on this one (hopefully i'm wrong). So it became a 'because i said so and i don't want to scare you so i'm not telling you why'. <br />I did let her watch her ballet class last week instead of making her participate. By the end of the lesson she decided on her own that maybe she could have participated. This week she went to class no problem. I let her learn by her own experience that she actually likes dance class. So i am testing his theory out.<br /><br />Good points: Kohn backs up his theory with lots of studies. He divides the book into sections so if you're just looking for how incentive programs work to motivate employees you can turn to that chapter. Ditto for school teachers and for parents.<br /><br />Unfortunately because the book is divided into sections above, it gets repetitious. hence 41/2 stars.<br /><br />fav quotes:<br /><br />P 250-1 Ryan and Deci have made an important distinction between 2 versions of internalization. In ‘introjection’… children swallow the rule whole. It is inside them but essentially unprocessed. It is possible to feel controlled from the inside as well as the outside; people sometimes pressure themselves in much the same way that they can be pressured by external events.’<br />	Internalization by itself- even the kind identified as introjection satisfies someone whose chief concern is to get a child to do something without the adult’s having to stand around prodding him with bribes and threats. Like a wind-up toy, a child who has introjected a particular value will stay in motion after the controller has left the scene. No wonder those who direct and profit from a particular … system prefer a ‘self-controlled—not just controlled—work force.’ 47 <br />	Ryan &amp; Deci argue persuasively tht we shold aim higher than this. [They propose] integration, [which] involves helping a child make the value her own, understand its rationale,and experience a sense of self-determination in acting in accordence with it. The objective here is a deeper experience of choice, one understood not just as a selection of Option A over Option B but as something’ anchored' in the sense of a fuller, more integrated functioning. 49. Adults can help children reach this goal by supporing their autonomy, giving them chances to solve their own problems… inviting them to participate in making meaningful decisions and engaging them in discussion about all of the above.<br />	 I [ A Kohn] think we want children not only to be deeply committed to our values and rules but to be capable of making their own decisions about which values and rules to embrace. Here, too, the best preparation for making decisions is practice at making decisions...Moreover, we will have to trust children at some point. This is a far cry from trying to implant a piece of ourselves in a child so he or she ‘voluntarily’ makes all the same decisions we would make.<br /><br />P 265 In another study, … supervisors were told that someone whose work they were overseeing either enjoyed the task or was doing it just for the money. Even tho these messages were generated randomly and bore no relation to the individual’s actual attitudes, the supervisors who believed they were in charge of someone who was extrinsically motivated responded by becoming more controlling. As a result, the employee did in fact end up less interested in the task, confirming the expectation about how one ‘has to' treat such employees. Conversely, the belief that a worker wasn’t just interested in getting a reward led supervisors to create the kind of work environment where the employee did come to enjoy the work more. 1<br /><br /><br /><br />1 Pelletier, L &amp; R. Vallerand. Supervisors’ Beliefs &amp; Subordinates’ Intrinsic Motivation. Journal of Personality and Social Psychology 71 (1996) 331-40<br /><br />252 In the final analysis, none of the virtues, including generosity and caring, can be successfully promoted in the absence of choice. A man recalled being "taught that my highest duty was to help those in need" but added that he learned this lesson in the context of the importance of "obeying promptly the wishes and commands of my parents, teachers, and priests, and indeed of all adults...Whatever they said was always right." <br /> The man who said this was Rudolf Hoess, the infamous commandant of Auschwitz. Prosocial values are important, but if the environment in which they are taught emhasizes obedience rather than autonomy, all may be lost.<br /><br /><br />Question: My daughter is afraid to try new foods (won't even touch them.) If i reward her for surmounting her fears is that the same as rewarding for something normal? See, i thot that getting something tangible helps her think about something other than how afraid she is. And it seems to work, too. She sees the goodies from the dollar store and wants to try a new food ( or retry a new food, since it takes more than 1 try to like a food, experts say) to get the coveted toy she's seen. She now eats vanilla yoghurt :)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12137798773407616353">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4456709">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9944162299422851571">A lot of what the author says - that the use of rewards as motivators (for children, students, employees, etc.) is not only ineffective, but often detrimental to morale and motivation - makes a lot of sense, and certainly represents a fresh perspective. Despite this, however, I couldn't shake a lingering feeling of disagreement. Not that I don't believe his arguments...but I also don't think he leaves enough room for individual difference. For example, while I do agree with him that intrinsic mo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9944162299422851571">A lot of what the author says - that the use of rewards as motivators (for children, students, employees, etc.) is not only ineffective, but often detrimental to morale and motivation - makes a lot of sense, and certainly represents a fresh perspective. Despite this, however, I couldn't shake a lingering feeling of disagreement. Not that I don't believe his arguments...but I also don't think he leaves enough room for individual difference. For example, while I do agree with him that intrinsic motivators are better in the long run than extrinsic ones, I can think of numerous times that rewards, praise, "A"s, etc. have effectively motivated me without killing a more native drive to succeed. Still, the book provides a lot of food for thought - and fodder for potentially productive argument- to anyone raising kids, teaching students at any level or managing employees. Certainly worth reading.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17682025">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2734969871761261963">As a college student, I had been very interested in Alfie Kohn's philosophies. After graduating and getting a job much sooner than expected, I decided to read this book. I am amazed by how much we control other people with rewards. I've never been a fan of behaviorism...in any form. Which doesn't make me widely popular as a teacher. My students were stunned when I took over and explained that I do not give treats for asking questions or learning. I also explained to them that they are not dogs a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2734969871761261963">As a college student, I had been very interested in Alfie Kohn's philosophies. After graduating and getting a job much sooner than expected, I decided to read this book. I am amazed by how much we control other people with rewards. I've never been a fan of behaviorism...in any form. Which doesn't make me widely popular as a teacher. My students were stunned when I took over and explained that I do not give treats for asking questions or learning. I also explained to them that they are not dogs and they don't get a treat for doing what they're asked. It's still a little rough at times, trying to keep order in a classroom with students who want rewards for doing so. I feel that so much character is built on intrinsic guidelines, it's too important to tamper with. I whole-heartedly agree with Kohn and love how he takes this book into life with adults as well as children. It puts me in a student's shoes---how do I feel about my boss trying to reward me into working hard when I would do that in the first place. What does it do to our work ethic, self esteem, and intrinsic motivation?!! I found this book very interesting and absolutely a MUST-READ!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2734969871761261963">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_53296840">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53296840" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/889182-beth-a" class="left imgcol" title="Beth A."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220544739p2/889182.jpg" alt="Beth A." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/53296840?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 08, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/889182-beth-a" class="user" title="Beth A.">Beth A.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Beth A. by: </span>
      Laura Dotson
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/889182-beth-a?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/889182-beth-a?shelf=parenting" class="actionLinkLite">parenting</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer53296840">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11424597557865914775">I love parenting books, and I love exploring different ideas on how to parent , but this one was more difficult for me. The first few chapters are based on the assumption that no one human has the right to control another person. The idea is abhorrent to Kohn. This may be true in the workplace, but for parenting and to a lesser degree schooling, there are times when even the most lenient parent must have some control. You can’t exactly reason with a two year old that running in the street isn’t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11424597557865914775">I love parenting books, and I love exploring different ideas on how to parent , but this one was more difficult for me. The first few chapters are based on the assumption that no one human has the right to control another person. The idea is abhorrent to Kohn. This may be true in the workplace, but for parenting and to a lesser degree schooling, there are times when even the most lenient parent must have some control. You can’t exactly reason with a two year old that running in the street isn’t safe and expect them to comply. Some control is necessary. Preferably some sort of physical boundary.<br /><br />The book became more interesting to me when he started discussing intrinsic and extrinsic boundaries. Kohn presented research that proves that when rewards are used to mold behavior (he calls it pop-behavioralism), although there may be an immediate change in behavior, when the rewards are removed, the behavior returns to pre-reward or worse. There are also many studies that show that when people are offered an award for a doing a difficult task they take longer or are less likely to accomplish the task than people not offered the reward. And when children were paid for doing a puzzle, they were less likely to play with it when they thought the study was over than someone who was not paid or rewarded. So his points were that rewards (and punishments) don’t work, and that they cause our motivation to become extrinsic. That is, instead of learning for enjoyment, one learns to please the teacher, get a sticker, or a grade. Instead of sharing because of an inner compassion, we share to please a parent or because we are praised for sharing. This makes sense to me. I want my children to be motivated by intrinsic motivation, to have and act upon values deeply rooted within themselves. But as a parent, trying to teach these values, when you take away rewards and punishment (which he states is even worse than rewards), what is left? Kohn seems to suggest discussing values and reasons with children. My son is not very verbal. I think I could talk all day and still not persuade him or get any idea why he objects to my request. I really feel that Kohn has taken away my parenting tools without giving me an adequate replacement. I don’t think that he provides enough examples of solutions that do work.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11424597557865914775">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_464835604">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/464835604" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2678799-lisa-delaine-youngblood" class="left imgcol" title="Lisa Delaine Youngblood"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1272318774p2/2678799.jpg" alt="Lisa Delaine Youngblood" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/464835604?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 27, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2678799-lisa-delaine-youngblood" class="user" title="Lisa Delaine Youngblood">Lisa Delaine Youngblood</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer464835604">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10988705755511948818">I have never read a book that so questioned societal norms, nor have I ever altered my views so much based on the concepts introduced in a book. The title of this book explains exactly what readers can expect. As with any book discussing parenting skills, work levels, and manipulation, readers will have to determine for themselves whether or not they can agree, disagree, or at least rethink their previous opinions. This book requires readers to look closely at the heart of motivations -- both in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10988705755511948818">I have never read a book that so questioned societal norms, nor have I ever altered my views so much based on the concepts introduced in a book. The title of this book explains exactly what readers can expect. As with any book discussing parenting skills, work levels, and manipulation, readers will have to determine for themselves whether or not they can agree, disagree, or at least rethink their previous opinions. This book requires readers to look closely at the heart of motivations -- both intrinsic and extrinsic -- and at the true outcomes that we wish to achieve. <br /><br />Because the concepts "rang true" for me, I began re-evaluating my parenting and managerial skills. I took most extrinsic rewards out of my dealings with my children, with my staff, and with our customers. As a library director I even insisted that we take prizes out of our summer reading clubs. I have been amazed at the positive results. Since that time my children have done much better in school. My staff members seem to give much more of themselves to their jobs. Our summer reading club enrollment and final numbers DOUBLED the first year. The main problems that I have had are when parents and teachers (though almost never children) seem unable to make the change.<br /><br />Unable to follow every single precept in the book, I do continue to dole out sporatic "good job" and "I appreciate that you ..." to acknowledge excellent work. :) <br /><br />Though thought provoking, the book is repetitious. It is broken into several sections, and readers may choose only to read the section or sections that partain to them. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10988705755511948818">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_105004503">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105004503" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1458233-marshall" class="left imgcol" title="Marshall"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Marshall" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/105004503?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 31, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1458233-marshall" class="user" title="Marshall">Marshall</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Parents, Teachers, Managers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Marshall by: </span>
      Joanie
  </div>



    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer105004503">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18148596219487275959">I didn't give this book a 5 for fantastic writing. Although, Kohn is funny and insightful at times but he is also kind of repetitive (if you only read the first 5 chapters you'd learn everything you needed to know about the problem with rewards). The ideas in this book rang true to me as I read them. For example, achieving short-term compliance from my kids by offering them rewards (go get ready for bed without a fight and I'll read you stories until 7:30) is not only manipulative and selfish mo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18148596219487275959">I didn't give this book a 5 for fantastic writing. Although, Kohn is funny and insightful at times but he is also kind of repetitive (if you only read the first 5 chapters you'd learn everything you needed to know about the problem with rewards). The ideas in this book rang true to me as I read them. For example, achieving short-term compliance from my kids by offering them rewards (go get ready for bed without a fight and I'll read you stories until 7:30) is not only manipulative and selfish motivated but undermining my long term goals of raising responsible, caring, self-motivated adults.<br /><br />I should warn you that I found this book disconcerting. I could see the disadvantages of rewards in my parenting and in the schooling I received but still not at all sure what I'm going to do about it (when I was trying to finish the book I kept offering my kids rewards to go away and let me finish in peace). However, it is already paying dividends with Eden who before this was going through what I was calling the "terrible threes" when in reality the problem was we were trying to parent as if she were 5 not 3.<br /><br />Jani's currently reading through a newer book of Kohn's called Unconditional Parenting. From our discussions it sounds like it has the same ideas repackaged and focused only on parenting. (Punished discusses schools and work rewards as well as parenting). So, if you're interested only parenting maybe you should try that one. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18148596219487275959">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1070144198">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1070144198" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/969751-jennifer" class="left imgcol" title="Jennifer"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1410964335p2/969751.jpg" alt="Jennifer" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1070144198?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 02, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/969751-jennifer" class="user" title="Jennifer">Jennifer</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1070144198">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4846095307359714382">The overarching premise of this book is that rewarding people can actually decrease the desired behavior. When people are manipulated by "if you do [x], then you get [y]" type rewards, the extrinsic motivator (the reward) starts to replace any intrinsic motivation the person might have towards the task and are much less likely to continue the behavior if/when the rewards stop. One quote that stuck out to me was that programs like "Book It!" (Pizza Hut) only serve to produce fat kids who hate rea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4846095307359714382">The overarching premise of this book is that rewarding people can actually decrease the desired behavior. When people are manipulated by "if you do [x], then you get [y]" type rewards, the extrinsic motivator (the reward) starts to replace any intrinsic motivation the person might have towards the task and are much less likely to continue the behavior if/when the rewards stop. One quote that stuck out to me was that programs like "Book It!" (Pizza Hut) only serve to produce fat kids who hate reading. <br /><br />The author applies this concept to both the work environment and also to education and parenting. I will admit that it has changed the way I've thought about interacting with my kids, making me think twice before offering the rote "Good job!" praise (a reward). <br /><br />The author goes on to talk about alternatives to rewards, which was my favorite part of the book. He talked about the Three C's - collaboration, content, and choice. Collaboration means actually working with people (children, employees) instead of just trying to manipulate them to get them to do what you want. Content means looking at what you're asking them to do. Is it reasonable? If you want a child to do something just because YOU SAID SO, and there's no real point to it, you should rethink what you're asking. Giving people real choice about what they study, what they work on, what they are intrinsically motivated to do creates buy-in and ownership. <br /><br />I applied these ideas to my current project. One of my coworkers was tasked with a tedious and uninteresting piece of work, and the return on investment was low. I realized that he had no collaboration, bad content, and no choice in the matter. He did, however, have some great ideas about improving the architecture of the code, and that work would have a very high ROI. I talked to the stakeholders of the system, pitched it to my manager, and got permission for us to shift our focus and do something that was both good for the client and infinitely more interesting for him. Now sometimes you don't have a choice about what you do at work. Uninteresting tasks need to get done - but don't assume that you have no choice. If you look for better options, ones that are more engaging and more enjoyable and more useful to the client, you might just find them. <br /><br />The author lost me when he started talking about grades being a reward. He seemed to think that children shouldn't be given grades in school... there are several reasons why I disagree, but it did bring up a lot of good discussion in my book club and was worth thinking about. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4846095307359714382">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_589138142">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/589138142" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1623181-kate" class="left imgcol" title="Kate"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Kate" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/589138142?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 13, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1623181-kate" class="user" title="Kate">Kate</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer589138142">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14781618353883356722">this book is so great in the one main thing it set out to do, which is to point out 1) how totally saturated our culture is in giving people rewards to act the way you want them to (behavioralism, a notion/technique popularized by BF Skinner but around for ages), and 2) to point out all the subsequent research showing how giving rewards for a task/attitude/behavior kills the positive relationship between the doer and the deed. If you start paying kids to play their favorite game, they lose inter</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14781618353883356722">this book is so great in the one main thing it set out to do, which is to point out 1) how totally saturated our culture is in giving people rewards to act the way you want them to (behavioralism, a notion/technique popularized by BF Skinner but around for ages), and 2) to point out all the subsequent research showing how giving rewards for a task/attitude/behavior kills the positive relationship between the doer and the deed. If you start paying kids to play their favorite game, they lose interest in the game, etc. Merit pay doesn't improve employee performance OR company performance. On and on. It's great stuff, and as a mom, has me looking at my own techniques for keeping the three year old in line. (Let's be real, there're lots of bribes and threats.)<br /><br />That said, if I read this book closely I feel like my head would explode. Not only is the prose cute and best sellers (which I honestly don't mind if the content is substantial), I found his repeated gotcha! thing irritating. And god damn is this repetitive. I mean, more than the usual repetitive idea-based, non-fiction best seller. So I read fast, skimmed many parts, all those passages where this man was reluctant to let go of the sound of his own voice, the hundred ways he makes the same point over and over, and... Okay, I"m not sure about this criticism because I was reading so fast, but I'm pretty sure there are more than a few dubious rhetorical/logical techniques. You know, when you use the conclusion as support for your conclusion. Or when you give readers false dichotomies. Or when you discredit opposing ideas entirely, make your point in a more refined way and... I pretty sure he gives his own arguments a nuance and qualifications which he doesn't give to opposing ones. <br /><br />That said, this book is perfect for a skim. Because the research about bahavioralism is unequivocal. And how much we (Americans) love to reward behavior we approve of--I mean, it doesn't even seem like an idea that has competing ideas. It just feels like what makes sense, because it's so ingrained in our life. And that is a powerful point that Kohn often makes very persuasively. <br /><br />I like the guy on Goodread's comment that this was the most interesting and the most boring book he's read in a while.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14781618353883356722">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer3740547622969041647">A great book. The book is written for two audiences, educators and business professionals. I only had to read the sections that pertained to education, but after reading them, I am curious to know if the business world is like the education world.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText3740547622969041647">A great book. The book is written for two audiences, educators and business professionals. I only had to read the sections that pertained to education, but after reading them, I am curious to know if the business world is like the education world. I will not praise Alfie for writing a well informed book, but simply acknowledge that it is. Teaching second grade, I can already see in 7yr olds how praise, rewards, and other behavior manipulators have altered there perspective of life. The "whats in it for me" mentality is a hard thing to break and to motivate students in the importance of actual learning instead of the main focus to get straight A's is equally difficult because so many parents are demanding just that. Alfie explains that we are training kids to grow up and be selfish and that the only reason to do something is to get something physical in return, because the act of doing a good deed because it might bring on a good feeling to oneself is a waste of time. I highly recommend this book to every educator in schools, day cares, churches, and parents. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_265947528">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265947528" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3074982-keith-kendall" class="left imgcol" title="Keith Kendall"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1261850552p2/3074982.jpg" alt="Keith Kendall" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/265947528?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 02, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3074982-keith-kendall" class="user" title="Keith Kendall">Keith Kendall</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3074982-keith-kendall?shelf=psychology" class="actionLinkLite">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer265947528">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10457304299121992895">By Alfie Kohn<br /><br />Alas the book challenges so many of our cherished beliefs that I am beginning to see the the loss of motivation in a new light. It is disconcerting to see how widely his critique applies.<br /><br /><br />"Skinner spent his life denying the idea of choice and urging us to control reinforcers in the environment since they, in turn, control us." (p. 30)<br /><br />The most notable aspect of a positive judgment is not that it is positive but that it is a judgment. (Page 102)<br /><br />The legendary statistical consultant W. E</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10457304299121992895">By Alfie Kohn<br /><br />Alas the book challenges so many of our cherished beliefs that I am beginning to see the the loss of motivation in a new light. It is disconcerting to see how widely his critique applies.<br /><br /><br />"Skinner spent his life denying the idea of choice and urging us to control reinforcers in the environment since they, in turn, control us." (p. 30)<br /><br />The most notable aspect of a positive judgment is not that it is positive but that it is a judgment. (Page 102)<br /><br />The legendary statistical consultant W. Edwards Deming, with his characteristic gift for understatement, has called the system by which Americans are praised and rewarded "the most power to inhibitor to quality and productivity in the Western world." He adds that it "nourishes short-term performance, annihilates long-term planning, builds fear, demolishes teamwork, nourishes rivalry and ... leaves people bitter." To this we can add that it is simply unfair to the extent that employees are held responsible for what are, in reality, systemic factors that are beyond their control. (Page 129)<br /><br /><br />Part 3 isn't as depressing as Part 1 was. There are no quick fixes; in fact, that is what makes behaviorism (rewards) so attractive. Rewards can be given to get the desired behavior without understanding the problem. In part 3 we are treated to recommendations not a few. Kohn recommends that really understanding why there is a problem is necessary to come up with an appropriate approach. I am now thinking about how I will "behave" differently because of reading this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10457304299121992895">...more</a>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17652266927765679618">As a self-motivated person who takes pride in a job well done, I've always felt that incentives at school and in the workplace served to undermine the value of my personal achievements. <span id="freeTextContainer6052364029022689883">It has been a while since I read Alfie Kohn's book. I heard him speak at a teachers' convention and was intrigued by his assertion that teachers and parents kill children's motivation by offering rewards/bribes. So, candy, stickers, certificates, cash and other incentives really don't motivate! It went along with my casual observation that students to whom I sent commendations soon lost their commendabe behaviors, and it went along with a university class I took in the 80's about motivational th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6052364029022689883">It has been a while since I read Alfie Kohn's book. I heard him speak at a teachers' convention and was intrigued by his assertion that teachers and parents kill children's motivation by offering rewards/bribes. So, candy, stickers, certificates, cash and other incentives really don't motivate! It went along with my casual observation that students to whom I sent commendations soon lost their commendabe behaviors, and it went along with a university class I took in the 80's about motivational theory. The idea is that providing rewards soon extinguishes a person's intrinsic motivation to learn or accomplish a task. I've seen criticisms that suggest Kohn's book is simplistic, but I think it is worth reading and considering.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1264951060">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1264951060?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 26, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16296434-alok" class="user" title="Alok">Alok</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1264951060">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12840453157229162869">I am a firm believer that the work obeys classical economics to first order. I personally take great enjoyment in maximising the value I receive from things. But I realise now that the reason I like to do it is because I enjoy the optimisation process and solving puzzles - often the benefit from the optimisation comes as a bit of a bonus.<br /><br />The central idea is that rewards decrease intrinsic motivation, reduce enjoyment of the thing itself. Since reading the book, I have stopped rewarding my kids f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12840453157229162869">I am a firm believer that the work obeys classical economics to first order. I personally take great enjoyment in maximising the value I receive from things. But I realise now that the reason I like to do it is because I enjoy the optimisation process and solving puzzles - often the benefit from the optimisation comes as a bit of a bonus.<br /><br />The central idea is that rewards decrease intrinsic motivation, reduce enjoyment of the thing itself. Since reading the book, I have stopped rewarding my kids for doing the things I want them to do. Instead, I focus on the intrinsically rewarding aspects of the things, and sometimes I don't get what I want. <br /><br />There is a parallel thread considering rewards at work. You can get really burnt out if you're constantly worrying how every little thing could affect your bonus or chances at promotion. After reading the book, I understand more about why rewards are so stressful- they quickly become equivalent to punishments. So the best strategy I have found is to try and forget about the rewards, and just focus on the doing the best job with the task at hand. I think that actually leads to me doing better work than if I keep the rewards in mind.<br /><br />Other ideas:<br /><br />When the person you report to determines your compensation, you have an incentive to conceal problems. Rather than asking for help which is a perquisite for optimal performance, you will be apt to spend your energies trying to flatter that person and convince them that you have everything under control. Very few things are as dangerous as a bunch of incentive driven individuals trying to play it safe.<br /><br />Performance appraisals allow supervisors to shift the responsibility for solving problems to their subordinates.<br /><br />Don't identify as pre-anything. The pre- indicates that what you are doing now doesn't matter except for what it produces later. And you shouldn't be doing things that don't matter.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12840453157229162869">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1127924784">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1127924784" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/23215047-elizabethe" class="left imgcol" title="Elizabethe"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Elizabethe" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1127924784?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/23215047-elizabethe" class="user" title="Elizabethe">Elizabethe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/23215047-elizabethe?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1127924784">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5210639158684136639">Everyone in America should read Part 1 of this book. This book was written in 1993 and revised in 1999, but it is still very influential and the culture Kohn argues against is still pervasive. Kohn's basic argument is that much of the way we attempt to incentivize people is based on a view of humanity informed by pop-behaviorism. Behaviorism fundamentally denies the personhood of humanity, seeing humans as nothing more than the sum total of their actions. There is no self, no soul, no conscience</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5210639158684136639">Everyone in America should read Part 1 of this book. This book was written in 1993 and revised in 1999, but it is still very influential and the culture Kohn argues against is still pervasive. Kohn's basic argument is that much of the way we attempt to incentivize people is based on a view of humanity informed by pop-behaviorism. Behaviorism fundamentally denies the personhood of humanity, seeing humans as nothing more than the sum total of their actions. There is no self, no soul, no conscience. You are what you do, and if you can be compelled to act in a different way then that's all that's needed to change your character. For anyone other than a strict materialist, this view of the human person should be problematic.<br /><br />In Part 1 of the book, Kohn goes through, in careful detail, study after study that refute the claim that rewards bring about a lasting change in behavior. Rewards, it seems clear, can discourage students, workers, and children from being interested in the very things they are supposed to encourage interest in. In short, extrinsic rewards do not motivate, motivation must come from within. More troubling is the possibility that attempting to incentivize behavior with rewards may backfire. Prizes for reading books seem to make students less inclined to read after the prize program is over, to cite just one example. Incentive pay seems to pretty clearly shown to not result in long term increases in productivity, prizes rewarded for being generous to others seem not to help people become more generous in the long run.<br /><br />Beyond this, however, Kohn's arguments are less compelling . There are three assumptions Kohn makes in attempting to offer broader advice based on carefully circumscribed evidence about rewards.<br /><br />1. He seems to replace the materialistic/pop behavioristic view of humanity with one that says human beings are naturally good creatures who are always rational actors instead of a view that sees that people all have a capacity for good and bad.<br />2. Kohn has an impoverished view of hierarchical relationships of any kind. His book does not admit that authority of one person over another could be based in anything but compulsion and inequality. So there's no thought that a child might develop obedience to a parent out of love and trust, that an employee might follow a manager or a business leader out of respect and admiration, that a teacher could inspire good behavior and a love of learning through leadership and respect. There's no idea that some could see honor in service or following orders or could wield authority over another person with love and a sense of responsibility for the good of that person.<br />3. Kohn does not seem to believe that there is an objective standard for "good behavior" that adults have a right to insist on from children. He thinks that most adults mean that "good behavior" is "behavior that doesn't bother the parent or teacher" and is always just a parent's personal preference.<br /><br />Kohn's assumptions lead him beyond what his evidence warrants in offering advice for replacing a system of rewards in punishments in the workplace, at school, and at home. For instance, the chapters that deal specifically with employment make the claim that questioning a system of rewards threatens free market capitalism. This is so far beyond what the evidence he presents suggests that I can only think he has a pre-existing ideological agenda. The few studies that he cites about the inefficacy of merit-based pay and incentive programs in compelling employees to act productively seemed compelling to me, but to extrapolate from that that it somehow undermines free markets and capitalism doesn't follow (at least, he doesn't spell out the steps). Kohn seems to me to show his bias by twice raising the question "isn't something other than employment rooted in merit-based pay communism?" and twice dismissing it as a question rooted in hysteria instead of an honest question deserving of a full and complete answer.<br /><br />The chapters on child-rearing are, quite simply, laughable. Parents should take seriously the critique of pop behaviorism and the potential pitfalls of rewards. But Kohn's suggestions for replacing them are, as I said above, based on a wholly negative view of the concepts of obedience and authority. He simply can't conceive that authority can be wielded with love, obedience asked for with a view towards a greater good, or that children could come to learn to obey their parents out of trust. Sometimes a parent must say, "we are going to do it this way because I know it is best for everyone and you are just going to have to trust me." and sometimes a parent may even say "because I said so" as shorthand for that explanation.<br /><br />As a homeschooling mother, I found his critique of the education system the most compelling. The school system we have -- based on a pop behavioristic model of children, made up of schools that are forced to teach to the test, that wield grades as rewards instead of feedback, that see the end of education as assigning a piece of paper that makes you eligible for a certain level of job or to get into a college instead of a love of learning, really is a problem. Of course, my view is that everyone who is concerned about this and takes it seriously should homeschool. If you can't do that, then you can try to find a school that is directed towards cultivating learning in children instead of asking them to jump through a series of educational hoops. That school should also have implemented a way to shield students from the effects of a national education standard that insists on incessant testing. If you can't do either of those things, you may find his advice on how to protect students from the pitfalls of our rewards-based education helpful.<br /><br />On my reading list is a book that might wholly rebut this one, Rewards, How to use them to Help Children Learn: and why teachers don't use them well, by Herbert J. Wahlberg. I'll edit this review when I have finished reading that.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5210639158684136639">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18348623204430479384">This book elaborates some research by Deci on motivation and how there can be counter-intuitive effects in some cases. I think there's a bit of a risk that the book tries to generalize a bit too much from too little. I'm basically a huge fan of the insight though and once you know about it, you'll start to see it everywhere and just have to shake your head. This obviously has enormous social import and so that's all the more reason to insist that we get it exactly right and make the most compelling case we can.<br /><br />Also, it's been a while since I've read it, but does it tie in at all with Maslow or Kohlberg or any sort of psychoanalytic or humanistic psychology? I seem to recall it was mostly just the one empirical result and the rest was just "and so this is why everything we do is wrong/backward" but it didn't really get into other psychology much. Too bad because I'd really love to read THAT book instead of just Deci plus applications/social criticism.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1306392975">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1306392975" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2578172-jamieanna" class="left imgcol" title="Jamieanna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1415541516p2/2578172.jpg" alt="Jamieanna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1306392975?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 07, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2578172-jamieanna" class="user" title="Jamieanna">Jamieanna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12150193-punished-by-rewards" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1306392975">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1512994614677011690"> I read Alfie Kohn’s Punished by Rewards: The Trouble with Gold Stars, Incentive Plans, A's, Praise, and Other Bribes which debunks the myth that rewards and punishments can effectively control and change human behavior. Kohn builds his argument by dismantling America’s love affair with“pop behaviorism,” which Arthur Koestler, author of The Act of Creation, describes as “For the anthropomorphic view of the rat, American psychology substituted a rattomoprhic view of man” (3). One of the most impo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1512994614677011690"> I read Alfie Kohn’s Punished by Rewards: The Trouble with Gold Stars, Incentive Plans, A's, Praise, and Other Bribes which debunks the myth that rewards and punishments can effectively control and change human behavior. Kohn builds his argument by dismantling America’s love affair with“pop behaviorism,” which Arthur Koestler, author of The Act of Creation, describes as “For the anthropomorphic view of the rat, American psychology substituted a rattomoprhic view of man” (3). One of the most important ideas of the book is after reviewing years of surprising research, Kohn illustrates how “carrots and sticks” have detrimental effects on workers’ and students’ performance and motivation, which seems counterintuitive to the popular belief. Kohn cites countless studies of elementary schoolers to college students and adults that show how rewards for completing tasks can singlehandedly annihilate quality, creativity and risk-taking. Throughout the book, Kohn presents this argument with a multitude of examples which he organizes in three contexts: the workplace, the school system, and parenting. A second important idea from this book is Kohn’s argument that praise is just as detrimental to motivation as tangible rewards and punishments. Kohn asserts, “We tell [students] how good they are and they light up, eager to please, and try to please us some more. There are the children we should really worry about” (104). This struck me as particularly eye-opening because I was one of those kids, and I am a PBIS teacher who is constantly trying to use more praise in my classroom! A third important idea from this book includes Kohn’s alternatives to using rewards and punishments. Kohn calls these alternatives “The Three Cs: Content, Collaboration, and Choice,” content meaning providing students with a genuine purpose and “compelling why” for what we do in the classroom, collaboration meaning true learning happening with what we do together rather than the control teachers use to “manage students,” and choice meaning the importance of providing real and meaningful choice and students' control in the classroom. Overall, this book really got me thinking about how to use more specific feedback of my observations of students instead of simply praising them, as well how I can possibly implement more standards-based grading practices even though my school district is light years away from this shift. Lastly, this book has a lot of substantial evidence disproving the effects of merit pay, which is frustrating when our profession’s compensation is headed in that direction. I strongly recommend this book; this was a very thought-provoking and detailed read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1512994614677011690">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1243393474" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5905798-leanna-aker" class="left imgcol" title="Leanna Aker"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311876320p2/5905798.jpg" alt="Leanna Aker" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1243393474?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5905798-leanna-aker" class="user" title="Leanna Aker">Leanna Aker</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1243393474">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4576136610588888787">So, I admit, I put this on my list because as a doctoral student in education, I feel I should have read this book. I did not dig Daniel Pink's Drive, which is a similar premise. However, this book rocked.my.world. I do not see how a teacher could read this book and not want to remove all of the silly token society reward systems they use in their classrooms. The book is compelling, cites multiple research studies (with integrity and critical thought), and gives practical suggestions to replace</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4576136610588888787">So, I admit, I put this on my list because as a doctoral student in education, I feel I should have read this book. I did not dig Daniel Pink's Drive, which is a similar premise. However, this book rocked.my.world. I do not see how a teacher could read this book and not want to remove all of the silly token society reward systems they use in their classrooms. The book is compelling, cites multiple research studies (with integrity and critical thought), and gives practical suggestions to replace rewards. Furthermore, the book treats the topic of rewards, praise, and the like realistically. Kohn admits that rewards work for very specific (and not very exciting or important) circumstances. I would highly recommend this to educators, parents, or managers looking to understand how to engage (or disengage) people.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer794726567">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11451380432802952793">Woah. This one is shocking in that it really challenges the current pop-psychology, behaviorism. Most of our society operates by punishments and rewards (commissions, awards, grades, detentions, sticker charts, etc.) This book challenges the idea that this is the best motivation or motivation at all for people. Kohn shows the research that backs this claim and goes the extra mile to show you how to implement or in some cases remove "carrots and sticks" from your environment be it business, perso</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11451380432802952793">Woah. This one is shocking in that it really challenges the current pop-psychology, behaviorism. Most of our society operates by punishments and rewards (commissions, awards, grades, detentions, sticker charts, etc.) This book challenges the idea that this is the best motivation or motivation at all for people. Kohn shows the research that backs this claim and goes the extra mile to show you how to implement or in some cases remove "carrots and sticks" from your environment be it business, personal, or educational. If this is the next step of social evolution, I'm ready for it. A world where people are not constantly judged good or bad and have intrinsic motivation to participate in life and their interests. I can't wait to learn more from Kohn and fellow like-minded people. I have already spread the message of this book and seen how difficult it is for some to consider. But have faith, I think that people possess more internal drive than we ever credit them for. In fact we don't need to give them credit for intrinsic motivation, that just decreases it!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14366773197654962986"><br /><br /> I have been wanting to read this for a long time and finally got all the way through it. After being a Communication Skills Instructor for 5 years and studying NVC and the like, this is right down my alley. I wish he hadn't spent so much trying to convince me.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText14366773197654962986"><br /><br /> I have been wanting to read this for a long time and finally got all the way through it. After being a Communication Skills Instructor for 5 years and studying NVC and the like, this is right down my alley. I wish he hadn't spent so much trying to convince me. I am pretty sure those who pick this book up have already questioned common rewards systems. Behaviorism has always rubbed me wrong, but I couldn't articulate why until I started studying communication. Humans have higher brain functions! We don't always operate in our immediate best interests (at least that is what I hope my parenting will accomplish) and we are capable of so much more. His ideas on how to solve the issue could have used more fleshing out, but it was a good beginning. The longer I read this, the more I wanted my little ones out of school. We took them back out and I don't feel quite as much cognitive dissonance. I wish this book was read and understood by more. I wish I didn't have to defend my detestation of debunked behaviorism every time I met for PT conference or therapy sessions with my daughter...</span>

  <a href="/user/show/24861-randall" class="left imgcol" title="Randall"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1245026384p2/24861.jpg" alt="Randall" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/813316462?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 31, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/24861-randall" class="user" title="Randall">Randall</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer813316462">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15845305528462416776">This book is a scathing indictment of common practices and beliefs that are pervasive in our society, and thereby radical, although the evidence, dutifully presented, shows that experts are increasingly aware of the problem.<br /><br />Kohn argues a strong case against rewards and punishments in all aspects of life, focusing on parenting, management, and teaching. The examples illuminate the pervasiveness of Skinnerian thinking in our society and the results of the experiments Kohn relies upon paint a truly</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15845305528462416776">This book is a scathing indictment of common practices and beliefs that are pervasive in our society, and thereby radical, although the evidence, dutifully presented, shows that experts are increasingly aware of the problem.<br /><br />Kohn argues a strong case against rewards and punishments in all aspects of life, focusing on parenting, management, and teaching. The examples illuminate the pervasiveness of Skinnerian thinking in our society and the results of the experiments Kohn relies upon paint a truly horrifying picture of what we are doing to ourselves if we remain ignorant.<br /><br />This may be a somewhat important read for any serious manager, teacher, or parent trying to cultivate pro-social values and intrinsic motivation.<br /><br />It may also be an important read for anyone feeling unmotivated or discontented themselves, as it might illuminate some of the causes and offers a way out, or at least an increase in compassion for the whole situation.<br /><br />Like many books translating scientific research for a lay audience this suffers a bit from excessive reiteration of its core ideas, but wrapped up quickly enough that I did make it through.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15845305528462416776">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_722352701">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/722352701" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2754669-liz-demar" class="left imgcol" title="Liz DeMar"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1295050491p2/2754669.jpg" alt="Liz DeMar" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/722352701?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2754669-liz-demar" class="user" title="Liz DeMar">Liz DeMar</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer722352701">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13021930991622680752">A bit verbose at times, particularly for the person already acquainted with Kohn's work. Kohn captivates by presenting study after study that demonstrate how rewards simply do not work. Pay for performance? Doesn't work. Grades for performance? Doesn't work. Bribery for child's behavior? Experience tells every parent that any benefits are fleeting. Not only do rewards not work, but ultimately they prove damaging. Offer a reward and you can watch productivity and creativity decline. People who ar</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13021930991622680752">A bit verbose at times, particularly for the person already acquainted with Kohn's work. Kohn captivates by presenting study after study that demonstrate how rewards simply do not work. Pay for performance? Doesn't work. Grades for performance? Doesn't work. Bribery for child's behavior? Experience tells every parent that any benefits are fleeting. Not only do rewards not work, but ultimately they prove damaging. Offer a reward and you can watch productivity and creativity decline. People who are offered rewards tend to choose easier tasks and become answer-oriented and often illogical in their problem-solving strategies.<br /><br />Kohn offers much insight into why rewards fail ("people only do what is necessary" and "people know when they're being controlled" being the two reasons that resonated with me personally) and he also offers advice on what to do instead of rewards. <br /><br />On a personal note, I have kept rewards very, very minimal with my own children (in large part because I read Kohn's "Unconditional Parenting" years ago). But I had become lax and allowed a few rewards, most notably rewarding my 5yo son for eating his dinner. Dinner was always a major contention. Not only did he refuse to eat, he would act almost belligerent throughout the meal. After I began reading this book I decided to completely stop our reward system as it was failing miserably anyway. Almost instantly he began eating and - more importantly - he became very pleasant during dinner once more. It was an interesting little case study that hopefully will help me to remember never to implement a reward system again.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13021930991622680752">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/5893960-shannah" class="user" title="Shannah">Shannah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer279303255">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer166653493685660494">I think everyone who interacts with students or employees ought to be required to read this book, since it debunks the myth of rewards as the best motivation at school or work. *spoiler alert* Skinnerian behaviorism is thoroughly discredited.<br /><br />At times it was hard to keep reading, since I kept getting freaked out about my kids and their schooling. I worry all the time that their joy in learning, their creativity, and their ability to make their own decisions in life are being crushed right out of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText166653493685660494">I think everyone who interacts with students or employees ought to be required to read this book, since it debunks the myth of rewards as the best motivation at school or work. *spoiler alert* Skinnerian behaviorism is thoroughly discredited.<br /><br />At times it was hard to keep reading, since I kept getting freaked out about my kids and their schooling. I worry all the time that their joy in learning, their creativity, and their ability to make their own decisions in life are being crushed right out of them (as they were out of me, both at home and at school). In the end, I persisted, mainly because at the end, Kohn provides practical ways to move away from the rewards and punishments I fall back on reflexively as I try to get my kids to do what I say (NOW!!). I am glad I did persist, because now I feel much, much better about the way I do things at home with my kids, and how I have (and will into the future) help them through difficult periods in their education. It is gratifying to know that the ways I have been doing things, in the main (and not when I am stressed out to the point of bribing or threatening my kids in desperation), are very much in line with what Kohn recommends. The book has helped me notice my own behavior with my kids, and recognize the positive things I do (explaining why I want them to do things around the house, or why they have to learn math/reading/spelling/whatever it is they don't like, or why we help our elderly neighbor, for instance) as well as the negative things (bribing, rewarding, withholding/punishments). I have been writing down times when I resort to "do this and you'll get that" -- and it has been a real eye opener.<br /><br />At any rate, I highly recommend you read this book as soon as you can. Your kids, co-workers, direct reports and everyone else you encounter, will be so much better off for it!<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="166653493685660494">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
