  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_130836351">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130836351" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1481730-kat" class="left imgcol" title="kat"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1264317457p2/1481730.jpg" alt="kat" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/130836351?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 20, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1481730-kat" class="user" title="kat">kat</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer130836351">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13641224019318872839">In a certain way, this was a really interesting book. Its main ideas were refreshing and surprising: Rewards demotivate people. Praise can do the same, plus insult them to boot.<br /><br />In another way, this was quite a boring book. The author seemed to have difficulty trusting that he had properly conveyed his points to the reader. The basic claims and the reasoning behind them were reiterated far too frequently. I kept wondering if the book had originally been published as separate articles for differen</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13641224019318872839">In a certain way, this was a really interesting book. Its main ideas were refreshing and surprising: Rewards demotivate people. Praise can do the same, plus insult them to boot.<br /><br />In another way, this was quite a boring book. The author seemed to have difficulty trusting that he had properly conveyed his points to the reader. The basic claims and the reasoning behind them were reiterated far too frequently. I kept wondering if the book had originally been published as separate articles for different magazines, written such that each chapter could be understood on its own without the reader's having absorbed the concepts in the previous chapters. The atrociously long sentences, with their many clauses and (often unnecessary) parenthetical asides, continually strained my attention to the breaking point. Despite my interest in the subject, I found myself daydreaming about picking up other books on my towering to-read list.<br /><br />The parts of this book that you don't want to miss involved the recaps of studies that back up Kohn's claims. These are fascinating: Children who were rewarded for playing certain math games avoided those same games like the plague when the rewards stopped coming, while children who had never been rewarded continued to play with and enjoy them. children who were praised for drinking an unfamiliar beverage showed less desire to have some more a week later compared to children who had consumed it without any adult comments. College students who were given a cash reward for playing with spatial puzzles stopped their play as soon as they were told the experiment was over (preferring to daydream or read the magazines in a holding room) while college students who had been promised no money at all kept tinkering with them. Preschoolers who were given a ribbon for drawing with Magic Markers showed less interest in drawing with the markers a week later.<br /><br />Kohn's explanation for these results is that offering a reward makes a person see whatever she's being reward for doing as an obstacle, something to be "over and done with" so that she can move on and get the goodie. Rewards suck the pure enjoyment out of a task and turn it into a chore. <br /><br />He also makes a convincing argument that using rewards is a vapid shortcut to compliance. If we offer a reward to keep a three-year-old from constantly getting out of bed, he says, then we are seeking compliance without considering WHY the child is doing this. He gives an example of this kind of "WHY" thinking: <br />"Maybe she's being put to bed too early and simply isn't sleepy yet. <br />Maybe she feels deprived of quiet time with her parents, and the evening offers the best opportunity for her to cuddle or talk with them. <br />Maybe she's still wound up from what happened a few hours earlier and needs to rehearse and clarify the day's events to try to make sense of what happened. <br />Maybe there are monsters under her bed. <br />Or maybe she can just hear people talking in the living room." <br /><br />This one passage, I think, is the most valuable part of the book. It's an excellent model of the kind of thinking he hopes will replace our knee-jerk impulse to reward. <br /><br />More examples like this would have helped answer the question the author says is frequently put to him, that of what to do "instead" of rewarding. He states that there is no one simple alternative to rewards, and I see what he means. But he still needed to provide more real-life examples for his readers. He needed to tell readers about specific classrooms whose teachers had made commitments to doing away with extrinsic motivation. He needed to tell us how it went for them, how the students reacted, which subjects were taught &amp; learned differently, whose interests or behaviors changed and in what ways, and he needed to include quotes so that we could hear what everyone involved had to say about it. He needed to include vignettes of how parents responded to X situation where most parents would have used bribes. The closest he comes to this is sharing vague quotes from letters he has received from teachers and parents stating that they'd "never go back" to rewards. <br /><br />What readers need is something concrete to hang onto, something positive to walk away with rather than the negative that most will have absorbed. _Punished by Rewards_ does a good job leaving readers with a prohibition: "DON'T Reward!" But it's a book that desperately needs a Part II.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13641224019318872839">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Lillard presents the research behind eight insights that are foundations of Montessori education, describing how each of these insights is applied in the Montessori classroom. But the federal mandate of &amp;quot;no child left behind&amp;quot; has come to mean curriculums driven by preparation for standardized tests and quantifiable learning results. Even for very young children, unstructured creative time in the classroom is waning as teachers and administrators are under growing pressures to measure school readiness through rote learning and increased homework. In her new book, Vivian Gussin Paley decries this rapid disappearance of creative time and makes the case for the critical role of fantasy play in the psychological, intellectual, and social development of young children.A Child\'s Work goes inside classrooms around the globe to explore the stunningly original language of children in their role-playing and storytelling. Drawing from their own words, Paley examines how this natural mode of learning allows children to construct meaning in their worlds, meaning that carries through into their adult lives. Proof that play is the work of children, this compelling and enchanting book will inspire and instruct teachers and parents as well as point to a fundamental misdirection in today\'s educational But is this the most effective method of motivation? No, says psychologist Edward L. Deci, who challenges traditional thinking and shows that this method actually works against performance. The best way to motivate people—at school, at work, or at home—is to support their sense of autonomy. Explaining the reasons why a task is important and then allowing as much personal freedom as possible in carrying out the task will stimulate interest and commitment, and is a much more effective approach than the standard system of reward and punishment. We are all inherently interested in the world, argues Deci, so why not nurture that interest in each other? Instead of asking, &amp;quot;How can I motivate people?&amp;quot; we should be asking, &amp;quot;How can I create the conditions within which people will motivate themselves?&amp;quot;&amp;quot;An insightful and provocative meditation on how people can become more genuinely engaged and succesful in pursuing their goals.&amp;quot; —Publisher\'s Weekly&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15843125-free-to-learn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/books/1372174032m/15843125.jpg" alt="Free to Learn: Why Unleashing the Instinct to Play Will Make Our Children Happier, More Self-Reliant, and Better Students for Life" />
</a>


<script>

We call this imprisonment schooling, yet wonder why kids become bored and misbehave. Even outside of school children today seldom&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText1118158090793608281\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;Our children spend their days being passively instructed, and made to sit still and take tests—often against their will. We call this imprisonment schooling, yet wonder why kids become bored and misbehave. Even outside of school children today seldom play and explore without adult supervision, and are afforded few opportunities to control their own lives. The result: anxious, unfocused children who see schooling—and life—as a series of hoops to struggle through.In Free to Learn, developmental psychologist Peter Gray argues that our children, if free to pursue their own interests through play, will not only learn all they need to know, but will do so with energy and passion. Children come into this world burning to learn, equipped with the curiosity, playfulness, and sociability to direct their own education. Yet we have squelched such instincts in a school model originally developed to indoctrinate, not to promote intellectual growth.To foster children who will thrive in today’s constantly changing world, we must entrust them to steer their own learning and development. Drawing on evidence from anthropology, psychology, and history, Gray demonstrates that free play is the primary means by which children learn to control their lives, solve problems, get along with peers, and become emotionally resilient. This capacity to learn through play evolved long ago, in hunter-gatherer bands where children acquired the skills of the culture through their own initiatives. And these instincts still operate remarkably well today, as studies at alternative, democratically administered schools show. When children are in charge of their own education, they learn better—and at lower cost than the traditional model of coercive schooling.A brave, counterintuitive proposal for freeing our children from the shackles of the curiosity-killing institution we call school, Free to Learn suggests that it’s time to stop asking what’s wrong with our children, and start asking what’s wrong with the system. It shows how we can act—both as parents and as members of society—to improve children’s lives and promote their happiness and learning.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"1118158090793608281\"     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/64319.Alfie_Kohn">About Alfie Kohn</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/64319.Alfie_Kohn"><img alt="64319" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1267597120p2/64319.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer2686283677955225533"> Alfie Kohn writes and speaks widely on human behavior, education, and parenting. The author of eleven books and scores of articles, he lectures at education conferences and universities as well as to parent groups and corporations.<br /><br />Kohn's criticisms of competition and rewards have been widely discussed and debated, and he has been described in Time magazine as "perhaps the country's most outspoken</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2686283677955225533"> Alfie Kohn writes and speaks widely on human behavior, education, and parenting. The author of eleven books and scores of articles, he lectures at education conferences and universities as well as to parent groups and corporations.<br /><br />Kohn's criticisms of competition and rewards have been widely discussed and debated, and he has been described in Time magazine as "perhaps the country's most outspoken critic of education's fixation on grades [and] test scores."<br /><br />Kohn lives (actually) in the Boston area with his wife and two children, and (virtually) at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.alfiekohn.org" target="_blank">www.alfiekohn.org</a>. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2686283677955225533">...more</a>

    </div>

				<p class="mediumText">No trivia or quizzes yet. <a href="/trivia/work/776112-punished-by-rewards-the-trouble-with-gold-stars-incentive-plans-a-s">Add some now »</a></p>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/776112-punished-by-rewards-the-trouble-with-gold-stars-incentive-plans-a-s">Quotes from Punished by Rewar...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“Some who support [more] coercive strategies assume that children will run wild if they are not controlled. However, the children for whom this is true typically turn out to be those accustomed to being controlled— those who are not trusted, given explanations, encouraged to think for themselves, helped to develop and internalize good values, and so on. Control breeds the need for more control, which is used to justify the use of control.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/439982-some-who-support-more-coercive-strategies-assume-that-children-will" class="actionLinkLite">20 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“In a word, learning is decontextualized. We break ideas down into tiny pieces that bear no relation to the whole. We give students a brick of information, followed by another brick, followed by another brick, until they are graduated, at which point we assume they have a house. What they have is a pile of bricks, and they don't have it for long.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/635095-in-a-word-learning-is-decontextualized-we-break-ideas-down" class="actionLinkLite">19 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a href="/work/quotes/776112-punished-by-rewards-the-trouble-with-gold-stars-incentive-plans-a-s" style="float: right" class="actionLink">More quotes…</a>
